No.  12.

COLUMBUS  C,  NASH  ET  AL.  (

Indictment  for  banding  and  conspiring  together  with  the  purpose  and
intent  to  prevent  and  hinder  citizens  of  the  United  States  in  the  free
exercise  and  enjoyment  of  the  rights  and  privileges  guaranteed  or  se-
cured  to  them  by the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the  United  States ;
and  for  willful  murder  while  so  conspiring  and  banded.

Certificate  of division  of opinion  and  writ  of error.

2

UNITED  STATES  OF  AMERICA,

District  of Louisiana:

Circuit  court  of  the  United  States,  fifth  circuit,  and  district  of  Louis-
iana.

Certificate  of division  in opinion.

At  a  circuit  courtof  the  United  States  in  and  for  the  district  of  Lou-
isiana,  begun  and  held  at  New  Orleans,  in  the  said  district  of Louisiana,
on  the  twenty-seventh  day  of  April,  in  the  year  of  our  Lord  one  thou-
sand  eight  hundred  and  seventy-four,  said  day being  the  commencement
of the  regular  April  term  of said  court  for said  year  of eighteen  hundred
and  seventy-four,  and  on  the  twenty-seventh  day  of  June,  A.  D.  1874,
said  last-mentioned  day  being  a  day  of  said  regular  term  of said  circuit
court,  the  following  proceedings  were  had  and  taken  in  said  court,  in
the  cause  of  the  United  States  of  America  against  Columbus  C.  Nash
et  al.,  as  appears  by the  record  thereof,  now  remaining  in  said  court,
duly  entered  of  record  by order  and  Cirection  of  the  court.

Present,  the  Hon.  Joseph  P.  Bradley,  associate  justice  of  the  Supreme

Court,  and  his  honor  William  Bb.  Woods,  circuit  judge.

3

Proceedings  on  motion  in  arrest  of judgment.

THE  UNITED  STATES  ?

vs.

-On  indictment  for  conspiracy,  &e.

No.  12.

C.  C.  NASH  AND  OTHERS.  §

This  was  an  indictment  against  the  defendants,  consisting  of  thirty-
two  counts,  a  copy  of which  is contained  in  the  record,  (prout  the  same.)
The  cause  was  tried  at  the  April  term,  1874,  and  three  of  the  defend-
ants,  William  Cruikshank,  J.  P.  Hadnot,  and  W.  B.  Irwin,  were  found
guilty  under  the  first  sixteen  counts,  and  not  guilty  as  to  the  others:  a
motion  for  a  new  trial  was  then  made  and  refused.

The  said  last  named  defendants  then  moved  in  arrest  of  judgment  as

foliows:

THE  UNITED  STATES
vs.
C.  C.  NASH  &  ALS.

{No 12.

U.S.  eircuit  5th  circuit  and  district  of  La.

Now  come  Wm.  J.  Cruikshank,  John  P.  Hadnot,  and  Wm.  D.  Twin,

IktEC.  602—1

2

THE  UNITED  STATES  V8.  CRUIKSHANK  ET  AL.

defendants  herein,  by their  counsel,  and  moved  the  court  to  set  aside
the  verdict,  to  arrest  the  judgment,  to  proceed  no  further  upon  the  ver-
dict  herein,  and  to  discharge  movers  upon  the  ground  following  appa-

rent  upon  the  face  of  the  record.

|

4

Ist.  Because  the  matters  and  things  set  forth  and  charged  in the
several  counts  of  the  indictment,  respectively,  numbers  one  to
sixteen,  inclusive,  upon  which  they  have  been  convicted,  do  not.  consti-
tute  offences  against  the  laws  of  the  United  States,  and  do  not  come
within  the  purview,  true  intent,  and  meaning  of  the  act  of Congress  ap-
proved  May  31,  1870,  entitled  “An  act  to  enforce  the  right  of  citizens  of
the  United  States  to  vote  in  the  several  States  of  the  Union,  and  for
other  purposes.”

2d.  Because  the  matters  and  things  inthe  said  indictment  set  forth
and  charged  do  not  constitute  offences  cognizable  in  this  honorable
court,  and  do  not  come  within  the  power  and  jurisdiction  of  this  hon-
orable  court.

3d.  Because  the  offences  created  by  the  6th  section  of the  act  of Con-
gress
sixteen
referred  to,  and  upon  which  section  the  aforesaid
counts  are  based,  are  not  constitutionally  within  the jurisdiction  of  the
courts  of the  United  States,  and  because  the  matters  and  things  herein
referred  to  are  judicially  cognizable  by  State  tribunals  only,  and  legis-
lative  action  thereon  is  among  the  constitutionalty  reserved  rights  of

the  several  States.

D

4th.  Because  the  said  act,  in  so  far  asit  creates  offences  and
imposes  penalties,  is  violative  of  the  Constitution  of  the  United
States,  and  an  infringement  of the  constitutionally  reserved  rights  of the
several  States  and  the  people.

Sth.  Beeause  the  eighth  and  sixteenth  counts  of  said  indictment  are
too  vague,  general,  insufficient,  and  uncertain  to  afford  accused  proper
notice  to  plead  and  prepare  their  defence,  and  set  forth  no  specific
offence  under  the  law.

|

6th.  Beeause  the  verdict  of the  jury  against  these  movers  is not  war-

ranted  or  supported  by  law.

And  thereupon,  upon  hearing  said  motion,  the  opinions  of  the  justice
and  judge  who  heard  the  motion,  to  wit,  Joseph  P.  Bradley,  associate
justice  ot  the  Supreme  Court,  and  William  b.  Woods,  cireuit  judge  of  the
fifth  cireuit,  were  opposed  in  the  question  whether  the  said  sixteen
counts  of said  indictment  are  severally  good  and  ‘sufficient  in Jaw,  and
contain  charges  of  criminal  matter  indictable  under  the  laws  of  the
The  opinion  of the  said  justice  being  that  the  said  sev-
United  States.
eral  counts  are  not,  and  that  neither  of  them  is, good and  sufti-
cientin  law,  and  do  not,  nor  does  either  of them,  contain  charges
of criminal  matter  indictable  under  the  laws  of the United  States:

G

and  the  opinion  of  the  said  circuit  judge  being  that  the  said  several  .
counts  are,  and  that  each  of them  is, good  and  sufficient  in law,  and  do,
and  each  of  them  does,  contain  charges  of  criminal  matter  indictable
under  the  laws  of the  United  States ;

|

And  the  opinions  of the  said  justice  and  judge  were  ‘also  opposed  on
the  question  whether  the  said  motion  in arrest  of judgment  should  be
granted,  the  said  justice  being  of opinion  that  it  ought  to  be  granted,
and  the  said  circuit  judge  being  of a  contrary  opinion.
*  Whereupon,  on  motion  of J. kh. Beckwith,  U.S.  atty.  for and  on  behalf
of the  United  States,  in  order  that  the  points  upon  which  the  disagree-
ment  hath  happened  may,  during  this  term,  be’stated,  under  the  direc-
tion  of the judges,  and certified  under the  seal  ofthe court,  to the Supreme
Court,  to  be finally  decided:  It  is  ordered  that  the  foregoing  state-

7

ment  of facts  and  points  of difference,  which  is made  under  the  di-
rection  of the judges,  besocertified  according  to the request  ofsaid
United  States  attorney,  acting  in  this  respect  for  and  in !behalf  of  the
United  States,  and  the law  in such  case  made  and  provided  ; and it is fur-
ther ordered  that  the clerk do further certify to the  Supreme  Court  in con-
nection  herewith  a  copy  of the  record  in  this  cause,  in  order  that  the

to  said  Supreme  Court.

Indictment  for  banding  and  conspiring  together  with  the  purpose  and
intent  to  prevent  and  hinder  citizens  of  the  United  States  in  the  free
exercise  and  enjoyment  of  the  rights  and  privileges  guaranteed  or
secured  by the  Constitution  and  laws of  the  United  States,  and  for
willful  murder  while  so  conspiring  and  banded.
Be  it remembered  that  on  the  9th  day  of  May,  A.  D.  1873,  at  the
April  term,  1873,  of the  said  court,  begun  and  held  at  the  city  of New
Orleans,  State  of Louisiana,  within  and  for  said  district,  on  the  fourth
Monday  of April,  A.  D.  1873,  the  following  proceedings  were  had  and
taken,  as  appears  by the  record  thereof,  as  follows,  to  wit:

vent  in  their  respective  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of their  lawful  right
and  privilege  to  peaceably  assemble  together  with  each  other  and  with
other  citizens  of  the  said  United  States,  for  a  peaceful  and  lawful  pur-
pose,  the  same  being  a  right  or  privilege  granted  or  secured  to  the  said
Levi  Nelson  and  Alexander  Tillman,  respectively,  in  common  with  all
other  good  citizens  of  the  said  United  States,  by  the  Constitution  and
laws  of the  United  States  of America,  contrary  to  the  form  of the  statute
of the  United  States  of  America  in  such  cases  made  and  provided,  and
against  the  peace  and  dignity  of  the  said  United  States.

fully  and  felomiously,  to  injure,  oppress,  threaten,  and  intimidate,  with
the  unlawful  and  felonious  intent,  thereby  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and
Alexander  Tillman,  then  and  there  respectively,  to hinder  and  pre-
vent  in  their  respectiv e  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of the  rights,
privileges,  immunities.  and  protection  granted  and  secured  to
them  respectively  as  citizens  of  the  said  United  States,  and  as  citi-
zens  of,  said  State  of  Louisiana,  by reason  of,  and  for  and  on  account
of  the  race  and  color  of  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  the  said  Alexander
Tillman,  and  for  the  reason  that  they, the  said  Levi  Nelson  and Alexander
Tillman,  being  then  and  there  citizens  of  the  said  State  and  of  the
said  United  States,  were  persons  of  African  descent  and  race,  and  per-
sons  of color,  and  not  white  citizens  thereof;  the  same  being  a  right  or
privilege  granted  or  secured  to  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  the  said  Alex-

the  said  United  States,  by  the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the  United

necut,  and  Prudhomme  Lemoine,  all  late  of  the  said  State  and  district
of  Louisiana,  being  persons  of evil  minds  and  dispositions,  together  with
divers  other  evil-disposed  persons  whose  names  are  to  the  grand  jurors
aforesaid  as  yet  unknown,  on  the  thirteenth  day  of  April,  in  the  year  of
our  Lord  one  thousand  eight  bundred  and  seventy-three,  at  the  parish  of
Grant,  in the  State  of Louisiana,  in  the  district  of Louisiana,  and  within
the  jurisdiction  of  said  court,  with  foree  and  arms,  unlawfully  and
feloniously  did  combine,  conspire,  and  confederate  together,  between  and
amongst  themse!ves,  for  and  with  the  unlawful  and  felonious  intent  and
purpose  one  Levi Nelson  and  one  Alexander  Tillman,  each  of said last-men-
tioned  persons  then  and there  being  acitizen  of the United  States of Amer-
ica,  of African  descent, and person  of color, then and there being in the peace
of the State  of Louisiana  and  of the  said  United  States,  unlawfally
and  feloniously  to  injure,  oppress,  threaten,  and  intimidate  with
the unlawful  and  felonious  intent,  thereby  the said Levi  Nelson  and
Alexander  Tillman, respectively,  then  and  there  to  hinder  and  prevent
in their  respective  and several  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of the rights,
privileges,  and  immunities  and  protection  granted  and  secured  to  them
respectively  as  citizens  of the  said  United  States,  and  citizens  of  the
said  State  of Louisiana,  by reason  of  the  race  and  color  of the  said  Levi
Nelson,  and  the  said  Alexander  Tillman,  and  because  for  the  reason
, that  they,  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  Alexander  Tillman,  being  then  and
there  citizens  of  said  State,  and  of said  United  States,  were  then  and
there  persons  of African  descent  and  race,  and  persons  of color,  and  not
white  citizens  thereof,  the  same  being  a  right  or  privilege  granted  or  se-
cured  to  the said  Levi  Nelson,  and  the  said  Alexander  Tillman,  respect-
ively,  in  common  with  all  other  guod  citizens  of the  said  United  States,
by the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the  United  States  of Ameica,  contrary  to
the  form  of  the  statute  of  the  United  States  of  America  in  such  case
made  and  provided,  and  against  the  peace  and  dignity  of the  said
United  States.

State  of Louisiana,  in  the  district  of Louisiana,  and  within  the  jurisdic-

band  together  with  the  unlawful  and  felonious  intent  and  purpose  one
Levi  Nelson,  and  one  Alexander  Tillman,  each  of  said  last-mentioned

of  African  descent,  and  person  of  color,  then  and  there  being  in  the

Levi  Nelson  and  Alexander  Tillman,  then  and  there  severally  and
respectively  to  prevent  and  hinder  in  their  free  exercise  and  enjoyment
of  their  several  and  respective  lawful  right  and  privilege  to  peaceably
assemble  together  with  each  other,  and  with  other  peaceable  citizens  of
the  said  United  States,  for  a  peaceable  and  lawful  purpose,  the  same
being  a  rightor  privilege  granted  or  secured  to the  said  Levi  Nelson  and
the  said  Alexander  Tillman,  respectively,  in  common  with  all other  good
citizens  of  the  said  United  States,  by the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the
United  States  of  America,  contrary  to  the  form  of  the  statute  of  the
United  States  of  America  in  such  case  made  and  provided,  and  against
the  peace  and  dignity  of the  said  United  States.

of law  therefor,  being  a  right  or  privilege  granted  or  secured  to  the  said

with  all  other  good  citizens  of  the  said  United  States,  by the  Consti-

State  and  district  of  Louisiana,  being  persons  of  evil  minds  and  dis-
positions,  together  with  divers  other  evil-disposed  persons  whose  names
are  to  the  grand  jurors  aforesaid  as  yet  unknown,  on  the  thirteenth  day
of  April,  in  the  year  of our  Lord  one  thousand  eight  hundred  and  sev-
enty-three,  at the  parish  of Grant,  in  the  State  of Louisiana,  in  the  dis-
trict  of Louisiana,  and  within  the  jurisdiction  of  said  court,  with  force
of arms  unlawfully  and  feloniously  did  combine,  conspire,  and  confeder-
ate  together,  between,  and  amongst  themselves,  for  and  with  the  un-
lawful  and  felonious  intent  and  purpose  one  Levi  Nelson  and  one
Alexander  Tillman,  each  of said  last-mentioned  persons  then  and
there  being  a  citizen  ofthe  United  States  of America,  of  African
descent,  and  person  of  color,  then  and  there  being  in  the  peace  of  the
State  of Louisiana,  and  of  the said  United  States,  unlawfully  and  felon-
iously,  to  injure,  oppress,  threaten  and  intimidate,  with  the  unlawful
and  feloniousintent  thereby  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  Alexander  Till-
man,  respectively,  then  and  there  to  prevent  and  hinder  in  their  re-
spective  and  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of their  several  and  respective
lawful  right  and  privilege  to  peaceably  assemble  together  with  each
other  and  with  other  citizens  of said  United  States,  for  a  peaceable  and
lawful  purpose,  the  same  being  a  right  or  privilege  granted  or  secured
to  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  the  said  Alexander  Tillman,  respectively,
in  common  with  all  other  good  citizens  of  the  said  United  States,  by
the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the  United  States  of America,  contrary
to  the  form  of the  statute  of the  United  States  of America  in  such  Case
made  and  provided,  and  against  the  peace  and  dignity  of  the  said»

United  States.

<A.N.  Spencer,  A.  Mason,  Napoleon  De  Brock,  Brenard  Dorsey,
L.  M.  McCain,  John  Torrey,  Oscar  Watson,  M.  Honnecut,  and
Prudhomme  Lemoine,  all  late  of  the  said  State  and  district  of Louis-
jana,  being  persons  of  evil  minds  and  dispositions,  together  with  di-
vers  other  evil-disposed  persons,  whose  names  are  to  the  grand  jurors
aforesaid  as  yet  unknown,  on  the  thirteenth  day  of  April,  in  the  year
of  our  Lord  one  thousand  eight  hundred  and  seventy-three,  at  the
parish  of Grant,  in  the  State  of  Louisiana,  in  the  district  of  Louisiana,
and  within  the  jurisdiction  of  said  court,  with  force  and  arms,  unlaw-
fully  and  feloniously  did  combine,  conspire,  and  confederate  together,
between  and  amongst  themselves,  for  and  with  the  unlawful  and  felon-
ious  intent  and  purpose  one  Levi  Nelson  and  one  Ale’ander  Tillman,
each  of said  last-mentioned  persons  then  and  there  being  a citizen  of the
United  States  of  America,  of  African  descent,  and  person  of  color,
then  and  there  being  in  the  peace  of  the  State  of  Louisiana  and  of the
said  United  States,  unlawfully  and  feloniously  to  injure,  oppress,
threaten,  and  intimidate,  with  the  unlawful  and  felonious  intent  there-
by  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  Alexander  Tillman,  respectively,  to  hin-
der  and  prevent  in  their  several  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of
all  the  rights  and  privileges,  immunities,  and  protection  granted
and  secured  to them,  respectively,  as  citizens  of  the  said  United
States  and  of the  said  State  of  Louisiana  for,  because,  and  on  account
of  and  by reason  of  the  race  and  color  of  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  the
said  Alexander  Tillman,  and  for  and  because  of  the  fact  that  the  said
Levi  Nelson  and  the  said  Alexander  Tillman,  then  and  there  being
citizens  of  the  said  State  of  Louisiana,  and  of  the  said  United  States,
were  then  and  there  persons  of African  descent  and  race,  and  persons  of
color  other  than  white,  the  same  being  a  right  or  privilege  granted  or
secured  to  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and  said  Alexander  Tillman,  respect-
ively,  in  common  with  all  other  good  citizens  of  the  said  United  States
by the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the  United  States  of  America,  con-
trary  to the  form  of the  statute  of  the  United  States  of America  in  such
case  made  and  provided,  and  against  the  peace  and  dignity  of  the  said
United  States.

the  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of their  severai  and  respective  right  and

G.  Estell,  A.  J.  Sapp,  J.  G.  Hanks,  C.  J.  Boatner,  C.  I’.  Campbell,
Charles  Crooks,  W.  8.  Dorsey,  J.  B.  Paul,  Emanuel  Paul,  Butler  Leng-
ingham,  Charles  Neely,  J.  C.  Thompson,  John  Snow,  A.  N.  Spencer,  A.
Mason,  Napoleon  De  Brock,  Brenard  Dorsey,  Prudhomme  Lemoine,  L.
M.  McCain,  John  Torrey,  Oscar  Watson,  and  M.  Honneeut,  all  late  of
the  said  State  and  district  of  Louisiana,  being  persons  of  evil  minds
and  dispositions,  together  with  divers  other  evil-disposed  persons  whose
names  are  to  the  grand  jurors  aforesaid  as  yet  unknown,  on  the
173  ~=s thirteenth  day  of  April,  in  the  year  of  our  Lord  one  thousand
eight  hundred  and  seventy-three,  at  the  parish  of  Grant,  in  the
State  of Louisiana,  in  the  district  of Louisiana,  and  within  the  jurisdic.
tion  of said  court,  with  force  and  arms,  unlawfully  and  feloniously,  did
combine,  conspire,  and  confederate  together,  between  and  amongst
themselves,  for  and  with  the  unlawful  and  felonious  intent  and  pur-
pose  one  Levi  Nelson  and  one  Alexander  Tillman,  each  of  said  last-
mentioned  persons  then  and  there  being  a  citizen  of the  United  States
of  America,  of  African  descent,  and  person  of color,  then  and  there
being  in  the  peace  of  the  State  of  Louisiana  and  of  the  said  United
States,  unlawfully  and  feloniously  to  injure,  oppress,  threaten,  and
intimidate  with  the  unlawful  and  felonious  intent,  thereby  the  said
Levi  Nelson  and  Alexander  Tillman,  then  and  their  respectively  to
hinder  and  prevent  in  their  several  and  respective  free  exercise  and
enjoyment  of  each,  every,  all,  and  singular  the  several  rights  and
privileges  granted  ‘or secured  to  the  said  Levi  Nelson  and the  said
Alexander  Tillman  respectively,  in common  with  all  other  good  citizens
of  the  said  United  States,  by  the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the
United  States  of  America,  contrary  to  the  form  of  the  statute
ot the  United  States  of America  in  such  case  made  and  provided,

174

and  against  the  peace  and  dignity  of the  said  United  States.

And  the  grand  jurors aforesaid,  on  their  oath  aforesaid,  do  further  pre-

sent:

That  the  said  L.  M.  MeCain,  John  Torrey,  Oscar  Watson,  M.  Honne-
cut,  Prudhomme  Lemoine,  Columbus  C.  Nash,  A.  V. Ragan,  W.  M. Wat-
son,  kh.  H.  Jackson,  C.  C.  Dunn,  D.  Hurdy,  J.  8. Payne,  B.  Tyson,  E. B.

Scharboro,  J.

win,  A.  Cruikshank,  J.  O.  Grayson,  M.  Nugent,  John  H.  Lewis,  Walter

Emanuel  Paul, Butler  Lengingham,  Charles  Neely, J.C.  Thompson,  John
Snow,  A.  N.  Spencer,  A. Mason,  Napoleon  De Brock,  and  Brenard  Dor-
Sey,  together  with  said  evil-disposed  persons  in  the  execution  of  said
last-mentioned  premises,  while  in  the  act  of  being  so  unlawfully  and
feloniously  combined,  conspiring  and  confederated  together  as  afore-
said,  with  and  for  the  felonious  intent  and  purpose  aforesaid,  and  while
in  the  actual  act  of  committing  the  crime  and  felony  aforesaid,  and  in
pursuance  of their  said  unlawful  and  felonious  combination  and  conspir-
ing,  intent,  and  purpose  aforesaid,  afterwards, to  wit:

At  the  said  parish  of  Grant,  in  the  State  and  district  of  Louisiana
aforesaid,  on  the  thirteenth  day of  April,  in  the  year  of  our  Lord  one
thousand eight  hundred  and  seventy-three,  with  force  and  arms,  at  and
within  the  district  aforesaid,  in  and  upon  the  body  of  the  said  Alexan-

176

der  Tillman,  in  the  peace  of the  State  of Louisiana  and  of the said »
United  States,  then  and  there  being  feloniously,  willfully,  and  of
their  malice  aforethought,  did  make  an  assault,  und  then  and
there  with  fire-arms  and  deadly  weapons,  which  they then  and  there  held
in  their  hands,  in  and  upon  the  body  of  him,  the  said  Alexander  Till-
man,  then  and there willfully  and  of their  respective malice  aforethought,
give and  inflict  a  mortal  wound,  of  which  said  mortal  wound  he,  the
said Alexander  Tillman,  then  and  there instantly  died ; and  so  the  jurors
aforesaid,  upon  their  oath  aforesaid,  say  that  the  said  Prudhomme  Le-
moine,  Columbus  C.  Nash,  A.  V.  Ragan,  W.  M.  Watson,  KR. E. Jackson,

defendants  on  trial  in  this  cause,  J.  P.  Hadnot,  Wm.  Cruikshank,  Den-
nis  Lemoine,  Prudhomme  Lemoine,  W.  B.  Irwin,  Clement  Penn,  Alfred
Lewis,  Thos.  Hickman,  also  being  severally  present  in  court,  as
well  as  William  R.  Whitaker  and  R.  H.  Marr,  their  several  coun-
sel,  and  J.  Rh.  Beckwith,  United  States  attorney,  also  then  and
there  being  present  in  court,  the  said  jury,  as  to  the  cause  of  the  said
defendant  Alfred  C,  Lewis,  indicted  herein  and  impleaded  by the  name
of  Alfred  Lewis,  did  then  and  there,  in  open  court,  through  their  fore-
man,  H.  Millspaugh,  render  and  deliver  the  following  verdict:

‘“  We,  the  jury,  find  the  said  Alfred  Lewis  not  guilty  in  manner  and

form  as  charged  in  the  said  indictment  herein.”

Whereupon  the  court  did  then  and  there  order  the  said  verdict  in
respect  and  in  behalf  of  the  said  defendant,  Alfred  Lewis,  to  be  so
recorded.

203

And  in  response  to  a  due  enquiry  by  the  court  in  that  behalf,  the  said
jury,  through  their  said  foreman,  and  severally,  did  report,  state,  and
find  and  aver  that  they  had  in  no  manner  agreed  upon  any  verdict
herein  as  to  the  said  defendants  J.  P.  Hadnot,  Wm.  Cruikshank,  Den.
nis  Lemoine,  Prudhomme  Lemoine,  W.  B.  Irwin,  Clement  Penn,  Thomas  .
Hickman,  and  Oscar  Givens,  on  trial,  and  that,  after  due,  careful,  and
protracted  deliberation  and  consideration,  it  was  impossible  for
said  jurors  to  agree  upon  and  find  and  return  any  verdict  as  to
said  last-named  accused  and  defendants,  and  that  no  verdict
could  result  from  any  further  consideration  and  deliberation  in  that
respect  and  behalf;  and  the  court  considering  the  said  report,  finding,
averment,  and  conclusion  of  the  said  jury  to  be true  in  manner  and  form
as  to  as  aforesaid  stated  by  the  said  jury,  through  its  foreman,  and  sev-
erally,  and  considering  it impossible  for  said  jury,  upon  further  delib-
eration,  to  agree  upon  and  render  any  verdict  in  this  cause  as  to  the
said  accused  and  defendants  J.  P.  Hadnot,  Wm.  Cruikshank,  Dennis
Lemoine,  Prudhomme  Lemoine,  W.  Bb.  Irwin,  Clement  Penn,  Thomas
Hickman,  and  Oscar  Givers,  or  as  to  or  concerning  either  of  them:

204

It  is  ordered  and  adjudged  that,  pursuant  to  the  provisions  of  the
statute  of  the  United  States  in  such  case  made  and  provided,  and  ot
the  law  and  the  premises,  and  in  order  that  the  ends  of  justice  may  be
attained,  the  said  jury  be,  and  are  hereby,  discharged  from  any  further
considerations  of  the  issues  in  the  cause  as  to  the  said  accused
defendants  J.  P.  Hadnot,  Wm.  Cruikshank,  Dennis  Lemoine,
Prudhomme  Lemoine,  W.  b.  Irwin,  Clement  Penn,  Thomas  Hick-
man,  and  Osear  Givens,  by reason  of their  said  disagreement  and  tnabil.
ity  to  agree  upon  a  verdict  upon  further  consultation  and  consideration
of  the  law  and  the  evidence  submitted  to  them,  and  that  the  proceed-
ings  in  this  cause  be  considered  and  adjudged  to  be  and  stand  as  a  mis-
trial  as  to  all  of said  last-named  defendants  and  accused,  and  that  they,
the  said  last-mentioned  defendants,  and  each  of  them,  be  remanded  to
the  custody  of  the  marshal  of  this  district,  in  order  that  this  cause  as
to  said  defendants  may  be  tried  and  determined  by  another  jury  in  due
form  and  course  of  law.

And  considering  the  said  verdict  of  the  jury  as  to  and  in  respect  of
the  said  defendant  Alfred  C.  Lewis,  herein  indicted  and  impleaded  as
and  by  the  name  of  Alfred  Lewis,  and  all  and  singular  the  premises
being  seen  and  fully  understood  by  the  court  now  here,  it  is  cor-
sidered  and  adjudged  by the  said  court  here  that  the  said  defendant

The  defendants,  on  trial  by  their  counsel,  Marr,  Whitaker,  Ellis  and

against  them  hereinafter  specified  and  numbered,  and  to  exclude
any  and  all  evidence  which  may  be  offered  in  support  of the  said
counts,  which  counts  are  numbered,  seperately,  1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 3, 9,

232

10,  11,  12,  13,  16,  17,  18,  19,  20,  21,  24, 25,  26,  27, 28,  29,  52,  on  the  fol-
lowing  grounds,  to  wit:

Ist.  Because  the  matter  &  things  set  forth  and  charged  in  the  above
numbered  counts  of  the  indictment  do  not  constitute  offences  against
the  laws  of  the  United  States,  and  do  not  come  within  the  purview  and
true  intent  and  meaning  of  the  act  of  Congress,  approved  May  3ist,
A.  D.  1870,  entitled  “An  act  to  enforce  the  right  of citizens  of  the  United
States  to  vote  in  the  several  States  of  the  Union,  and  for  other  pur-
poses. ”

2nd.  Because  the  matter  and  thingsin  said  numbered  counts  set  forth
and  charged,  do  not  constitute  offences  cognizable  in  this  honl.  court,
and  do  not  come  within  the  power  and  jurisdiction  of  this  honl.  court.

ord.  And  defendants  further  move  to  exclude  all  evidence  ofiered  in
support  of the  killing  charged  in  the  counts  of the  indictment,  upon  the
ground  that  the  7th  section  of  the  said  statutes  of  May  lst.  1870,  does
not  affix  the  penalty,  but  refers  for  the  same  to  the  laws  of  the
States  respectively,  which  are  not  uniform;  and  that,  in  this  re-
gard,  said  section  is  in  violation  of  the  Constitution  of  the  United
States,  it  not  being  equal  and  uniform  in  its  operations  upon  all  citizens
of  the  United  States.

two  counts,

This  was  an  indictment  against  the  defendants,  consisting  of  thirty-
acopy  of  which  is Contained  in  the  record,  (prout  the  same.)
The  cause  was  tried  at  the  April  term,  1874,  & three  of  the  defendants,
William  Cruikshank,  J.  P.  Hadnot,  and  W.  6.  Irwin  were  found  guilty
under  the  first  sixteen  counts,  and  not  guilty  as  to the others.  A  motion
for  a  new  trial  was  then  made  and  refused.

The  said  last-named  defendants  then  moved  an  arrest  of judgment,  as

follows:

ad  24

ToE  UNITED  STATES  }

C.C.  Nasi  &  ALS.

\

ws,

.  Nc,  endl

U.S.  circuit  court,  oth  circuit  &  district  of  La.

Now  come  Win.  J.  Cruikshank,  John  P.  Wadnot,  and  Win.  LB.  Irwin,
defendants  herein,  by their  counsel,  and  move  the court  to  set  aside  the
verdict,  to  arrest  the judgment,  to  proceed  no  further  upon  the  verdict
herein,  and  to  discharge  movers,  upon  the  ground  following,  apparent
—  the  face  of  the  re cord.

Ist.  Because  the  matters  &  things  set  forth  and  charged  in  the  sev-
eral  counts  of  the  indictment,  respectively  numbers  one  to  sixteen,
inclusive,  upon  which  they  have  been  convicted,  do  not  constitute
offences  against  the  laws  of  the  United  States,  and  do  not  come  within
the  purview,  true  intent,  and  meaning  of the  act  of Congress,  approved
May  3ist,  1870,  entitled  “An  act  to  enforce  the  right  of citizens  of  the
United  States  to  vote  in  the  several  States  of  the  Union,  and  for  other
purposes.

Ynd.  Because  the  matters  &  thipgs  in  the  said  indietment  set  forth
and  charged  do  not  constitute  offences  cognizable  in  this  honor-
able  court,  and  do  not  come  within  the  power  and  jurisdiction  of
this  honorable  court.

srd.  Because  the  offences  created  by the  6th  section  of the  act  of Con-
eress  referred  to  and  upon  which  section  the  aforesaid  sixteen  counts
are  based,  are  not  constitutionally  within  the  jurisdiction  of the  courts
of  the  United  States,  and  because  the  matters  and  things  therein  re-
ferred  to  are  judicially  cognizable  by  State  tribunals  only,  and  legisla-
tive  action  therein  is among  the  constitutionally  reserved  rights  of  the
several  States.

4th.

Beeause  the  said  act,  in  so  far  as  it creates  offences  &  imposes
penalties,  is  violative  of  the  Constitution  of  the  United  States,  and  an
infringement  of  the  constitutiovally  reserved  rights of the  several  States
and  the people.

Sth.  Because  the  eighth  and  sixteenth  counts  of  said  indictment  are
too  vague,  general,  insufficient,  and  uncertain  to  afford  accused  proper
notice  to  plead  and prepare  their  defence,  and  set  forth  no specific offence
under  the  law.

6th.  Beeause  the  verdict  of the  jury  against  these  movers  is  not  war-

ranted  or  supported  by law.

23%

And  thereupon,  upon  hearing  said  motion,  the  opinions  of  the

THE  UNITED  STATES  Vs.  CRUIKSHANK  ET  AL.

83

justice  and  judge  who  lteard  the  motion,  to  wit,  Joseph  VP.  Brad-
ley,  associate  justice  of  the  Supreme  Court,  and  William  B.  Woods,
circuit  judge  of  the  fifth  circuit,  were  opposed  on  the  question  whether
the  said  sixteen  counts  of  said  indictment  are  severally  good  and  sufii-
cient  in  law,  and  contain  charges  of  criminal  matter  indictable  under
the  laws  of the  United  States;  the  opinion  of.the  said  justice  being  that
the  said  several  counts  are  not,  and  that  neither  of  them  is,  good  and
sufficient  in  law;  and  do  not  nor  does  either  of  them  contain  charges
of  criminal  matter  indictable  under  the  laws  of  the  United  States ;
and  the  opinion  of  this  said  circuit  jadge  being  that  the  said  several
counts  are,  and  that  each  of them Is,  good,  and  suffici ent  in  law,  and  do
and  each  of  them  does  contain  charges  of  criminal  matter  indictable
under  the  laws  of  the  United  States.

And  the  opinions  of  the  said  justice  and  judge  were  also  opposed
on  the  question  whether  the  said  motion  in  arrest  of  judgment
should  be  granted,  the  said  justice  being  of  opinion  that  it  onght
to  be  granted,  and  the  said  cirenit  judge  being  of  the  contrary

255

opinion.

Whereupon,on  motion  of J.  R.  Beckwith,  U.  S.atty.  for  and  in  behalf,
of the  United  States,  in  order  that  the  points  upon  which  the  disagree-
ment  hath  happened  may  during  this  term  be  stated  under  the  direc-
tion  of  the  judges,  and  certified  under  the  seal  of  the  court,  to  the  Su-
preme  Court,  to  be  finally  decided,  it  is  ordered  that  the  foregoing
statement  of  facts  and  points  of  difference,  which  is made  under  the  di-
rection  of  the  judges,  be  so  certified  according  to  the  request  of  said
United  States  attorney  acting  in  this  respect  for and  on  behalf  of  the
United  States,  and  the  I: uw  in  such  case  made  &  provided.

And  it  is  further  ordered  that  the  clerk  do  farther  certify  to  the  Su-
preme  Court,  in  connection  herewith,  a  copy  of  the  record  in  this  cause,
in  order  that  the  facts  involved  in  said  disagreement  may  fully  appear

and  be  manifest  to  said  Supreme  Court.

239

And  the  opinion  of the  honorable  Joseph  P.  Bradley,  presiding
judge,  being  that  the  said  motion  in  arrest  of  judgment  was  and
is  well  taken  and  good  and  sufficient  in  law,  and  should  prevail,  pursu-
ant  to  the  statutes  of the  United  States  in such  case  made  and  provided,
whereby,  in  such  case  of disagreement,  the  opinion  and  jadgment  of  the
presiding  judge  shall  prevail,  it 1s  ordered  that  judgment  herein  upon
the  verdict  of  the  jury  as  recorded  be,  and  the  same  is  hereby,  arrested.
And  on  motion  of  the  said  U.  8S.  atty.,  it  is  ordered  that  the  said
United  States  of America  be, andis  hereby,  allowed  a  writ  of error  to the

Marshal’s  return.

Received  July  15th,  1874,  by the  U.  S.  marshal,  and  on  the  3rd  day
of  August,  1874,  served  William  J.  Cruikshank,  William  D.  Irwin,  and
John  P.  Hadnot,  within  named,  each  with  this  citation,  by handing  the
same  to  each  of  them  in  person,  at  the  town  of  Alexandria,  Rapides
Parish,  La.,  560  miles  from  New  Orleans.
William  hk.  Whitaker,  their
counsel  of  record,  I served  with  this  citation  on  the  13th  of  July,  1874,
by  handing  the  same  to  him  in  person,  in  this  city.

S.  B.  PACKARD,

OF

No.  609.

IN  ERROR  TO  THE  CIRCUIT  COURT  OF  THE  UNITED  STATES  FOR  THE
DISTRICT  OF  LOUISIANA.

brief  for  the  United  States.

This  is  a  certificate  of  division  between  Mr.  Justice
Bradley  and  Judge  Woods,  while  holding  the  circuit
court  above  mentioned,  at  April  term,  1874,  upon  a
motion  to  arrest  judgment,  a  verdict  of  guilty  having
been  found  upon  the  first  sixteen  counts  in  the  indiet-
ment.

The  indictment  (returned  June  16,  1873,)  is  for  con-
spiracy,  under  the  sixth  section  of  the  Enforcement  act
of  May  30,  1870,  which  is  as  follows:

‘That  if  two  or  more  persons  shall  band  or  conspire
together,  or  go  in  disguise  upon  the  public  highway,  or
upon  the  premises  of another,  with  intent  to  violate  any
provisions  of  this  act,  or  to  injure,  oppress,  threaten,  or
intimidate  any  citizen  with  intent  to  prevent  or  hinder
his  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of any  right  or  privilege

1 CR

)

granted  or  secured  to  him  by  the  Constitution  or  laws
of  the  United  States,  or  because  of his  having  exercised
the  same,  such  persons  shall  be  held  guilty  of  felony,
and,  on  conviction  thereof,  shall  be  fined  or  imprisoned,
or  both,  at  the  discretion  of  the  court—the  fine  not  to
exceed  five  thousand  dollars,  and  the  imprisonment  not
to  exceed  ten  years  ; and  shall,  moreover,  be thereafter
ineligible  to,  and  disabled  from  holding,  any  office  or
place  of  honor,  profit,  or  trust  created.  by the  Constitu-
tion  or  laws  of  the  United  States.”

(16 Stat.,  141.)

(As  the  prosecution  was  commenced  before  the  Re-
vised  Statutes  went  into  efiect,  it  is  necessary  only  to
add  that  the  provisions  of  the  above  section  are  to  be
found  in  section  5508  of  that  work,  (p.  1018;)  the  only
substantial  change  being  that  the  offense  is  no  longer  a
felony.
That  this  repeal  and  re-enactment  (Rev.  Stat.,
sec.  5596)  do  not  affect  the  prosecution  and  punishment
of  this  offense,  see  Revised  Statutes,  sect.  5598. ]

The  counts  upon  which  the  verdict  was  found  com-
prise  two  series  (of eight  counts  each)  differing  from
each  other  only  in  that  those  of  the  first  series  charge
that  the  defendants  banded  together,  while  those  of  the
second  charge  that  they  combined,  conspired,  &c.  They
are  to  be  found  in  the  record,  from  p.6  to  p. 21,  bottom.
The  counts  in  each  series  differ  among  themselves
in  specifying  the  object  of  the  conspiracy,  as  being  the
hinderance  and  prevention  of certain  citizens  of African
descent,  &c., from  (1) peaceably  assembling  together,  (2)
bearing  arms,  (5) enjoying  life  and  liberty,  &e.

Upon  the  motion  in  arrest,  “‘the  opinions  of  the  jus-
tice  and  judge,  who  heard  the  motion,  to  wit,  &c., were
opposed  in  the  question  whether  the  said  sixteen  counts
of  said  indictment  are  severally  good  and  sufficient  in

o

law,  and  contain  charges  of  criminal  matter  indictable
under  the  laws  of  the  United  States:  The  opinion  of
the  said  justice  being  that  the  said  several  counts  are
not,  and  that  neither  of  them  is,  good  and  sufficient  in
law,  and  do  not,  nor  does  either  of  them,  contain
charges  of  criminal  matter  indictable  under  the  laws
of  the  United  States;  and  the  opinion  of  the  said  cir-
cuit  judge  being  that  the  said  several  counts  are,  and
that  each  of them  is, good  and  sufficient  in  law,  and  do,
and  each  of  them  does,  contain  charges  of  criminal
matter  indictable  under  the  laws  of  the  United  States;
And  the  opinions  of  the  said  justice  and  judge  were
also  opposed  on  the  question  whether  the  said  motion
in  arrest  of judgment  should  be  granted;  the  said  jus-
tice  being  of  the  opinion  that  it ought  to  be  granted,
and  the  said  circuit  judge  being  of a  contrary  opinion.

In  order  to  relieve  the  discussion  of  difficulties  peeu-
liar  to  some  of  the  counts,  which,  practically,  (U.S.
v.  Pirates,  5 Wheat.,  154,)  are  extraneous  to  the  issue
between  the  United  States  and  the  defendants,  we  will
confine  ourselves  to  the  14th  and  16th  counts,  (begin-
ning  at  the  bottom  of  pp.18  and  20  of  the  record,)
which,  as  intimated  above,  are  in  effect  the  same  as
counts  6 and  8 of the  first  series.

The  substance  of these  counts  Is:
14.  That  the  defendants,  [and  others,]  on  the  13th
of April,  1873,  at  the  parish  of  Grant,  in  the  State  of
Louisiana,  feloniously  conspired,  one  L.  N.  and  one  A.
T., each  a  citizen  of  the  United  States,  of African  de-
scent,  and  a person  of color,  *  to injure,  oppress,  threaten,
and  intimidate,”  with  the  intent  such  citizens  ‘to  pre-
vent  and  hinder  in  their  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of

¢
é

Per
MO

a

4

their  several  and  respective  right  and  privilege  to vote
at any election  to  be thereafter,  according
to law, had  and
held  by the  peoplein  and  of the  said  State  of Louisiana,
or  by the  people  of  and  in  the  said  parish,”  &c.;  the
defendants  well  knowing  the  said  citizens  to  be  well
qualified  and  entitled  to  vote  at  any  such  election  as
aforesaid,  contrary  to  the  form  of the  statute,  &e.

16.  This  count  corresponds  with  that  above  down  to the
words  prevent  and  hinder,  after  which  it proceeds  thus:
‘‘in  their  several  and  respective  free  exercise  and  enjoy-
ment  of  each,  every,  all  and  singular  the  several  rights
and  privileges  granted  or  secured  to  the  said  L.  N.  and
the  said  A.  T.  in  common  with  all  other  good  citizens
of the  said  United  States  by the  Constitution  and  laws  of
the  United  States  of America,  contrary  to  the  form,”  &e.

One  question,  therefore,  presented  to  this  court  is,
whether  persons  who  have  been  found  guilty  of conspir-
ing to prevent  certain  duly-qualified  citizens  from  voting
at  any  election  in the  State  of Louisiana,  (which  expres-
sion,  of  course,  includes  all  Federal  elections  in  such
State),  and  also  guilty  of  conspiring  to  prevent  such
citizens  from  exercising  any  one  of  the  rights  and  priv-
ileges  granted  or  secured  to  them  by  the  Constitution
of  the  United  States,  are  guilty  of  a  crime  of which  the
United  States  can  take,  and  has  taken,  cognizance.
Besides  this,  there  is  a  question  whether  the  counts  con-
taining  the  charges  are  in  due  technical  form.

We  will  endeavor  to  maintain  (1)  that  one  or  both  of
the  above  counts  contain  a  charge  of a  crime  cognizable
by  the  United  States,  in  the  court  in which  it was  tried ;
(2)  that  such  counts  are  sufficient  in  form.

.

It  is not  necessary  to  detain  the  court  by  discussing
The  charge  therein  is also
the  14th  count  at  length.
comprised  under  the  general  language  of  the  16th  count.
We  will  therefore  give  our  special  attention  to  the  mat-
ters  alleged  in  the  latter.

I.  The  crime  charged is cognizable  by the  United  States,

in  the  court  in  which  it was  tried.

A  conspiracy  thay  be  an  offense  against  the  United
States.
Ifit  be  to  do  something  which  directly  con-
cerns  the  United  States,  and  if  it  have  been  made  erim-
inal  by statute,  it is  within  Federal  jurisdiction.

Although  it  be  to  do  something  which  directly  con-
cerns  the  United  States,  it  is  not  within  Federal  crimi-
nal  jurisdiction  unless  itself  made  criminal  by’  statute,
as  there  is  no  common  law  of  crime  under  the  United
States.

sut  where  Congress  has  enacted  that  a  combination
to  effect  an  object  which  directly  concerns  the  United
States  shall  be  criminal,  nothing  material  occurs  to  us
to  suggest  that  such  legislation  is  not  both  competent
and  proper.
There  are  several  instances  of  such  legis-
lation;  one  of  which  is  of long  standing,  (3 Mareh  1825,
§ 25,  4  Stat.,  115,)  has  been  often  enforced,  and  pever
seriously  questioned  from  the  present  point  of view,  so
far  as  we  are  informed.
(See,  also,  acts  of 31 July,  1861,
§1,  and  of March  2, 1867,  § 50;  12  Stat.,  284,  and  14  Stat.,
471.)

3y the  section  quoted  above  from  the Enforcement-law,
so  far  as  material  here,  Congress  has  enacted  that  if tio
or  more  persons  shall  band  or  conspire  together  with  intent
to  injure,  oppress,  threaten,  or  intimidate  any  citizen  with

ae agepanneg
eas  Agee?

intent  to prevent  or  hinder  his free  exercise  of any  right  or
privilege  granted  or  secured  to  him  by the  Constitution  or
laws  of the  United  States,  such  person  shall  be held  guilty  of
Felony,  &e.

We  might  content  ourselves  lie With  saying  that
inasmuch  as  the  XI Vth  amendment  to the  Constitution
establishes  the  relation  of  citizenship  cf  the  United
States  independently  of  any  other  citizenship,  and  con-
fers  upon  Congress  the  power  to  enforce  that  relation
by suitable  legislation,  it seems  piain  that  if  such  citi-
zenship  be  not  merely  a  sound,  signifying  nothing ; if it
include  (ex.  gr.)  political  and  other  “rights  and  privi
leges”  respecting  the  Government  of  the  United  States,
this  amendment  empowers  and  thus  makes  it the  duty  of
Congress  to  legislate  so  as  to  enforce  those  rights  and
privileges,  whether  by  protection  against  criminal  as-
sault  or  otherwise.

This is  an  express  and  a  fresh  signification  of the  will
of  the  people  of the  United  States  upon  this  point,  but
itis  not  the  origin  of  that  duty,  for  it  seems  that  this
statute  would  have  been  constitutional  at  any  time  since
1789,  as  regards  (ex.  gr.)  the  right  to  vote  in  Federal
elections;  the  right  to  resort  to  the  protection  of  Fed-
eral  courts;  the  right  to  hold  Federal  offices;  the  right
to  inter-state  and  foreign  commerce  ;  and  the  right  to as-
semble  and  consult  about  Federal  polities,  and to  petition
Congress  for  redress  of Federal  grievances.
(Slaughter-
house  cases,  16  Wall.,  p.  79.)

|

As  these  rights  owe  their  existence  to  the  Federal  Gov-
ernment,  and  as,  vice  versa,  the  Federal  Government
owes  its  value,  if  not  its  existence,  very  much  to  their
exercise,  it  seems  that  nothing  can  be  said  to  impeach
the  validity  of Federal  legislation  to protect  them  against

7

any  opposer.
(See  United  States  vs.  Quinn,  8 Blatch.,
48;  and  the  Opinion  of Mr. Justice  Bradley  in the present
case,  below,  Am.  Law  Reg.,  Oct.,  1574.)
Such  protec-
tion  seems  indeed  to  raise  not  so  much  a  question  of
POWER  in  the  United  States,  as  of DUTY.

As  an  illustration:  The  right  to  vote  in  Federal  elec-
tions  is entirely  a Federal  right,'and  cases  affecting  it must
be cases  under  the  Federal  Constitution.
It  makes  no  dif-
ference  that  the  Constitution  of  the  United  States  has
chosen  to  define  this  right  by referring to a  definition  in
the several  State  constitutions.
The  latter  definition  is,
the  reference,  taken  to  be  incorporated  into  the  United
States  Constitution,  and  cannot  affect  the  character  of
the  vote  as  a  Federal  right.

This  right  is  entitled  to  all  the  guaranties  which  the
Jederal  Government  can  afford  to any  right  which  it be-
stows.  We  mean  no  offense  by comparing
it, forinstance,
with  the  well-known  Iederal  right  to  reclaim  fugitives
from  labor;  but  we  assert  its  equality  with  that  right
only  to  avail  ourselves  of what  has  been  said  in connec-
tion  with  the latter  by this  court,  in  the  case  of Prigg  vs.
Pennsylvania,  16  Pet.,  539.

After  stating  that  the  constitutional  provision  that
fugitives  from  labor  shall  be  delivered  up  gives  rise  to
many  questions,  such  as,  By  whom?  In  what  mode?
How  enforced  ? &e.,  the  court  proceeds  :

These  and  many  other  questions  will  readily  occur  upon  the
slightest  attention  to  the  clause;  and  it is obvious  that  they  can
receive  but  one  satisfactory  answer.
They  require  the  aid  of  legis  -
Jation  to  protect  the  right,  to  enforce  the  delivery,  and  to  secure  the
subsequent  possession  of the slave.  If,indeed,  the  Constitution  gnar-
anties  the  right,  and  if  it requires  the delivery  upon  the  claim  of  the
owner,  (as  cannot  well  be  doubted,)  the  natural  inference  certainly
is,  that  the  National  Government  is  clothed  with  the  appropriate

NE NR Te Cl haha

8

authority  and  functions  to  enforce  it.  The  fundamental  principle,
applicable  to  all  cases  of  this  sort,  would  seem  to  be  that  where
the  end  is  required  the  means  are  given;  and  where  the  duty  is
enjoined,  the  ability  to  perform  it  is contemplated  to  exist  on  the
part  of  the  functionaries  to  whom  it is  intrusted.
The  clause  is
found  in  the  National  Constitution,  and  not  in  that  of  any  State.
It  does  not  point  out  any  State  functionaries,  or  any  State  action  to
The  States  cannot,  therefore,  be
carry  its  provisions  into  eftect.
compelled  to  enforce  them;  and  it  might  well  be  deemed  an  uncon-
stitutional  exercise  of  the  power  of  interpretation  to  insist  that
the  States  are  bound  to  provide  means  to carry  into  effect  the  duties
of  the  National  Government  nowhere  delegated  or  intrusted  to
On  the  contrary,  the  natural,  if  not  the
them  by  the  Constitution.
necessary,  conclusion  is,  that  the  National  Government,  in  the
absence  of  all  positive  provisions  to  the  contrary,  is bound,  through
its  own  proper  Departments,  legislative,  judicial, or executive, as
the  case  may  require,  to  carry  into  effect  all  the  rights  and  duties
imposed  upon  it  by  the  Constitution.

The  remark  of  Mr.  Madison,in  the  Federalist,  (No.  43,)  would
seem  in  such  cases  to  apply  with  peculiar  foree:  “A  right,’  says  he,
‘‘implies  a  remedy  ;  and  where  else  would  the  remedy  be  deposited
than  where  it  is  deposited  by  the  Constitution?”  meaning,  as  the
context  shows,  in  the  Government  of  the  United  States.

It  is plain,  then,  that  where  aclaim  is  made  by  the  owner,  out
of  possession,  for  the  delivery  of  a  slave,  it  must  be  made,  if  at  all,
against  some  other  person;  and  inasmuch  as  the  right  is a  right  of
‘property,  capable  of  being  recognized  and  asserted  by proceedings
before  a  court  of justice,  between  parties  adverse  to  each  other,  it
constitutes,  in  the  strictestsense,  a  controversy  between  the  parties,
and  a  case  ‘‘  arising  under  the  Constitution”  of  the  United  States,
within  the  express  delegation  of judicial  power  given  by that  in-
strument.
Congress,  then,  may  call  that  power  into  activity  for
the  very  purpose  of  giving  effect  to  that  right;  and,  if  so,  then  it
may  prescribe  the  mode  and  extent  in which  it shall  be  applied,  and
how  and  under  what  circumstances  the  proceedings  shall  afford  a
complete  protection  and  guarantee  to  the  right.

*

*

*

But  it has been  argued  that  the  act  of Congress  is unconstitutional,
because  it does  not  fall  within  the  scope  of  any  of  the  enumerated
powers  of legislation  confided  to  that  body;  and  therefore  it is void.
Stripped  of  its  artificial  and  technical  structure,  the  argument

5

No  one  has

comes  to  this,  that  although  rights  are  exclusively  secured  by,  or
duties  are  exclusively  imposed  upon,  the  National  Government,  yet,
unless  the  power  to  enforce  these  rights  or  to  execute  these  duties
can  be  found  among  the.  express  powers  of  legislation  enumerated
in  the  Constitution,  they  remain  without  any  means  of giving  them
effect  by  any  act  of  Congress;  and  they  must  operate  solely  proprio
vigore,  however  defective  may  be  their  operation;  nay,  even  al-
though,  in  a  practical  sense,  they  may  become  a  nullity  from  the
want  of  a  proper  remedy  to  enforce  them  or  to  provide.  against
their  violation.
If  this  be  the  true  interpretation  of  the  Con-
stitution, it must,in  a  great  measure,  fail  to  attain  many  of  its
avowed  and  positive  objects,  as  a  security  of  rights,  and  a  re-
cognition  of  duties.
Such  a  limited  construction  of  the  Con-
stitution  has  never  yet  been  adopted  as  correct,  either  in  theory
or  practice.
ever  supposed  that  Congress  could,
constitutionally,  by  its  legislation  exercise  powers  or  enact  laws
beyond  the  powers  delegated  to  it  by  the  Constitution.
But
it  has,  on  various  occasions,  exercised  powers  which  were  necessary
and  proper  as  means  to  carry  into  effect  rights  expressly  given
and  duties  expressly  enjoined  thereby.
The  end  being  required,  it
has  been  deemed  a  just  and  necessary  implication  that  the  means
to  accomplish  it  are  given  also;  or,  in  other  words,  that  the  power
flows  as  a  necessary  means  to  accomplish  the  end.
Thus,  for  ex-
ample,  although  the  Constitution  has  declared  that  Representatives
shall  be  apportioned  among  the  States  according  to  their  respective
Federal  numbers,  and,  for  this  purpose,  if  has  expressly  authorized
Congress,  by. law,  to  provide  for  an  ennmeration  of  the  population
every  ten  years,  yet  the  power  to  appoition  Representatives  after
this  enumeration  is  made  is  nowhere  found  among  the  express
powers  given  to  Congress,  but  it  has  always  been  acted  upon  as
irresistibly  flowing  from  the  duty  positively  enjoined  by the  Con-
stitution.
Treaties  made  between  the  United  States  and  foreign
powers  often  contain  special  provisions,  which  do  not  execute
themselves,  but  require  the  inte:  position  of Congress  to  carry  them
into  effect,  and  Congress  has  constantly,  in  such  cases,  legislated
on  the  subject  ; yet,  although  the  power  is  given  to  the  Executive,
with  the  consent  of  the  Senate,  to  make  treaties,  the  power  is
nowhere  in  positive  terms  conferred  upon  Congress  to  make  laws
It  has  been
to  carry  the  stipulations  of  ,treaties

into  efiect.

aie

BE A
PO LLOLEL
ae ——_ — 7 LED

10

supposed  to  result  from  the  duty  of  the  National  Government
to  fulfill  all  the  obligations  of  treativs.
The  Senators  and  Rep-
resentatives  in  Congress  are,  in  all  cases,  except  treason,  felony,
and  breach  of  the  peace,  exempted  from  arrest  during  their  at-
tendance  at  the  sessions  thereof,  and  in  going  to  and  *returning
from  the  same.  May  not  Congress  enforce  this  right  by  author-.
izing  a  writ  of  habeas  corpus,  to  free  them  from  an  illegal  arrest
If  it  may  not,
in  violation  of  this  clause  of  the  Constitution?
then  the  specific  remedy  to  enforce  it  must  exclusively  depend
upon  the  local  legislation  of  the  States;  and  may  be  granted
or  refused  according  to  their  own  varying  policy  or  pleasure.  The
Constitution  also  declares  that  the  privilege  of  the  writ  of  habeas
corpus  shall  not  be  suspended  unless  when,  in  cases  of  rebellion  or
invasion,  the  public  safety  may  require  if.  No  express  power  is
given  to Congress  to secure  this  invaluable  right  in the  non-enumer-
ated  cases,  or  to  suspend  the  writ  in  cases  of  rebellion  or  invasion.
And  yet  it would  be  difficult  to  say,  since  this  great  writ  of  liberty
is usually  provided  for  by the  ordinary  functions  of legislation,  and
can  be  effectually  provided  for  only  in  this  way,  that  if  ought  not
to  be  deemed  by necessary  implication  within  the  scope  of the  legis-
lative  power  of  Congress.

We  adopt  this  argument,  and,  applying  its  doctrines
to  the  right  to  vote  in  Federal  elections,  we  conclude
(may  it not  be said  a fortiorz  ?) that  it iscompetent  for the
United  States  to  protect  that  right  by the  exercise  of a!
its  functions,  legislative,  executive,  and  judicial;  and
that  such  competency  does  not  spring  from  the  recent
constitutional  developments,  but  is coeval  with  the  ear-
liest  adoption  of the  Constitution—has  at all  times  been
a  part of its very jibre—and,  as  regards  importance,  may
be  said  to  be  an  article  of standing  or  falling  sovereignty.
Whilst  it may  be  that  it is only  because  of  the  recent
Amendments  that  persons  of the  African  race  share  in
this  secondary  right,  (t. e.,  of protection,  &e.,)  the  right
itself  is  as  old  as  any  of  those  primary  rights  for  which
it may  be  vouched.

5

The  above  section  of  the  Enforcement  act  is, there.
fore,  an  ample  warrant  for  the  prosecutions  which  it
commands  by  way  of  protection  of  Federal  rights  and
privileges.  How  far  its  provisions  cover  the  case  before
the  court  will  be  discussed  in  connection  with  what  we
have  to  say  about  the  form  of  the  counts.

It  has  been  suggested  that  by the  third  section  of the
Jivil-rights  act  of  1866,  (14  Stat.,  27,)  which  was  re-
enacted  by the  eighteenth  section  of  the  Enforcement
act,  (16  Stat.,  144,)  jurisdiction  over  such  prosecutions
is vested  exclusively  in  the  several  District  courts  of  the
United  States.  We  refer  to  the  case  of  United  States
vs.  Holliday,  5  Wall,  407,  as  showing  that  the  Circuit
courts  have  in  such  cases  concurrent  jurisdiction.  (See
Rev.  Stat.,  sec.  629,  clause  20.)

Il.  The  14th  and  16th  counts  (involving  also  counts  6

and  8) are  sufficient  in form.

The  question  involved  here  concerns  only  statutory
conspiracy,  and  as  the  authorities  in  regard  to  certainty
in  criminal  pleading  turn  a  great  deal  upon  the  consid-
eration  whether  the  pleading  concerns  a  common-law
crime,  or  only  one  that  is  of  statutory  creation;  and
whether  it be  for  a  crime  actually  committed,  or  for  a
mere  conspiracy,  we  will  confine  ourselves  to  a  compari-
son  of  the  certainty  exhibited  by the  counts  in question,
with  the  standard  of that  quality  required  by the  author-
ities  in  cases  of  Conspiracy  and  of  Statutory  crimes.

1.  We  first  call  attention  in  general  to  the  8th  section
of  the  act  of  1872,  ch.  255,  (17  Stat.,  198,)  viz:  *  That
no  indictment  found  and  presented  by a  grand  jury  in
any  district  or  circuit  or  other  court  of  the  United
States  shall  be  deemed  insufficient,  nor  shall  the  trial,

(Rev. Stat.,  see.  10205.)

2.  Irrespective  of  the  above  statute,  there  seems  to
be  due  certainty,  of  venue,  of  time  and  place  of  the  con-
spiracy ; of the parties  against  whom  the  conspiracy  was
made, as  tothe  status  of thelatter  as  citizens  of the United
States,  &c.,  and  of  conclusion.

3.  In  other  respects,  the  charge  is, in  nideahiinn,  that
the  defendants  conspired  with  the  intent  one  L.  N.,  do. to
injure,  oppress,  threaten,  and  intimidate,  with  intent  the
said  L. N.,  &e.,  to  prevent  and  hinder  in  their  several  and
respective  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of each  and  every,
all  and  singular,  the  several  rights  and  privileges  granted
and  secured  to  the said  L.  N.,  &c.,  in common  with  all other
good  citizens  of the  said  United  States,  by the  Constitution
and  laws  of the  United  States,  Ce.

The  corresponding  provisions  of  the  statute  are,  If
two  or  more  shall  conspire  with  intent  to  injure,  oppress,  |
threaten,  or  intimidate  any  citizen,  with  intent  to  prevent
or  hinder  his  free  exercise  and  enjoyment  of any  right  or
privilege  granted  or  secured  to  him  by the  Constitution  or  -
laws  of the  United  States,  such  person  shall  be  held  guilty
of felony,  &e.

It  appears  by  inspection  that  the  words  of the  statute
have  been  pursued  very  closely  in  the  indictment.
This
is in  accordance  with  the  rule  of  pleading  in  such  cases
as  laid  down  in  all  the  authorities.

Wharton  tells  us:
It  is  a  well-settled  general  rule,  that  in  an  indictment  for  an
offense  created  by  statute,  it  is  sufficient  to  describe  the  oftense
in  the  words  of  the  statute;  and  if,  in  any  case,  the  defendant

15

insists  upon  a  greater  particularity,  it  is  for  him  to  show  that,
from  the  obvious  intention  of  the  legislature,  or  the  known  prin-
ciples  of  law,  the  case  falls  within  some  exception  to  such  gen-
eral  rule.
Where
But  few  exceptions  to  this  rule  are  recognized.
the  words  of  the  statute  are  descriptive  of  the  offense,  the  in-
dictment  should  follow  the  language,  and  expressly  charge  the
described  offense  on  the  defendant,  or  it  will  be  defective.
It  is
necessary  that  the  defendant  should  be  brought  within  all  the  ma-
terial  words  of:the  statute;  and  nothing  can  be  taken  by intend-
ment.”  (Criminal  Law,  vol.  1, sec.  364.)

We  also  cite Bishop on  Statutory  Crimes,  sees.  575,  &e.;
Bishop  on  Crim.  Proced.,  2d  ed.,  Secs.  608,  612,  and  sees.
23, &c;  in  the  last  of  which  books,  as  cited,  is  con-
tained  a  full  discussion  of  the  rule,  and  also  of  the  ex-
ceptions  thereto.
Whiting  vs.  The  State,  14  Conn.,  487  ;
and  State  vs.  Lockbaum,  38  Conn.,  400;  Mason  vs.  The
State,  42  Ala.,  545;  State  vs.  Cooke,  38 Vt.,  457;  United
States  rvs.  Gooding,  12  Wheat.,  460,  and  United  States
vs.  Mills,  7  Pet.,  138.

The  present  is  a  case  of  statutory  felony,  but  this  per-
haps  makes  no  difference  under  the  provisions  of  the
act  of  1872  above  quoted,  (see  United  States  rvs.  Staats,
How.,  41,)  and  probably  never  made  a specific  difference,
beyond  that  of  rendering  necessary  the  use,  in  some
form,  of  the  word  felonious.
(Regina  vs.  Gray,  9  Cox
Cr.  Cases,  417;  Erle’s  Case,  2  Lewin,  133.)

The  application  of the  above  rule  and  itsexception  isto
bedetermined  in  great  measure  by precedents:  bearing
in  mind  a  general?application  of  the  rule,  that  the  cer-
tainty  required  in  indictments  is such  as  serves  to  iden-
tify  the  issue  upon  trial  with  that  passed  upon  by  the
grand  jury, to  inform  the  defendant  of what  it  is  that  he
is  to  answer,  to  warrant  the  jury  in  its  finding  upon  the
premises,  and  the  court  in  its judgment  thereupon,  and

also,  in  case  the  defendant  is  put  on  trial  a  second  time

We  submit  that  in  the  present  case  the  statute  ex-
presses  the  facts  which  make  the  offense,  and,  therefore,
all  that  is  necessary  to  state  in  the  indictment.

The  case  is one  in  which  the  statute  has  itself  created
and  defined  the  offense,  and  therefore  precedents  of  in-
dictments  upon  statutes  which  affect  offenses  already
existing,  or  which,  in  describing  the  offense,  use  words
of  legal  art  at  common  law,  are  not  in  point.
(See
Bishop,  Stat.  Crimes,  see.  331.)

a.  The  rights,  a  conspiracy  to  hinder  whose  exercise
is  rendered  indictable  by  the  statute  before  us,  are  de-
scribed  therein  as  “any  rights  or  privilege  granted  or
secured  to  him  by the  Constitution  or  laws  of the  United
States.”

The  sixteenth

count  of  the  indictment  before  us
charges  that  the  defendants  conspired  against  each  and
every,  all  and  singular,  of  such  rights  and  privileges  of
the  citizens  named.

A  conspiracy  haying  a  vast  object  must  nevertheless
be  described  in  its  own  likeness,  and  does  not  thereby
become  liable  to  censure  as  vague;  as  in  cases  where
indictments  charge  a  conspiracy  to  cheat  indefinite  in-
dividuals  or  the public.
See
also  Harley’scase,  7 Met.,  506, and  2d Bishop,  Cr. Proced.,
1866,  173.

(Judd’s  case,  2 Mass.,  329.

It follows,  indeed,  from  Harley’s  case  that,  if the  count
had  charged  that  the  conspiracy  was  against  some
named  one  of the  rights  and  privileges  of  Nelson,  &c.,
proof  of a  conspiracy  against  all  such  rights  would  not

15

have  supported  it.  The  conspiracy  must  be  charged
as  it  was  in  fact,  and  not  as  what  it  was  in  legal  con-
clusion.

3

It is  quite  conceivable  that  a  conspiracy  may  have  had  |
an  object  of  no  less  extent  than  that  here  charged.
There  may  be  couspiracies  to  do  what  may  be  called
political  murder,  t. e.,  to  deprive  a  citizen  of  all  his  po-
litical  rights,  as  well  as  conspiracies  to  affect  political
Each  of  these  is
mayhem,  or,  so  to  say,  mere  battery.
contemplated,  and  is  covered  by the  statute.
Its  words
protect  the  rights  referred  to, severally  and  collectively.
If the  indictment  before  us  had  charged  that  the  eon-
spiracy  was  aimed  at  two  specified  rights,  no  fault  in
pleading  would  have  been  committed,  a!though  the  bur-
den  of  proof  for  the  prosecution  might,  perhaps,  lave
been  greater  than  if  it  had  specified  only  one,  and  at
the  same  time  the  defense  rendered  correspondingly
more  easy.
This  is  true  (and,  as  to  the  latter  part  of
the  proposition,  in  greater  degree)  of  a  case  in  which
all  of  those  rights  are  involved.
To  include  in  a  de-
scription  “  all”  of such  aclassof  objects,  is as  specific  as
to  mention  only  any  named  one  of  them.
The  burden
upon  the  prosecution  may  be  greater,  but  it seems  plain
that  the  notice  to  the  defendant  of what  he is  to  answer
is, in  a  legal  sense,  entirely  definite,  and  that  the  basis
for  a  subsequent  proof  of  a  former  conviction  or  acquit-
tal  is all  that  is  necessary.
All  that  he  will  need  upon
a  plea  of former  conviction,  &c.,  is to  show  that  the  for-
mer  proceedings  involved  the  matter  at issue  in  the  lat-
ter,  which  is always,  to  as  great  an  extent  as  here,  to be
determined  by  parol  proof  of  what  previously  trans-
pired  in  the  evidence.  Wharton,  Cr.  Pl.,  (1868,)  secs.
569,  &e.

16

If it be suggested  that  the  9th  section  of the  act  of 1872,
ch.  255, (17 Stat.  at  L., 198;  Rev.  Stats.,  sec.  1035,)  by pro-
viding that  adefendant  may  be found  guilty  of any  offense
necessarily  included  inthat  with  which  he is  charged,  nul-
lifiesthis  argument  ina great  degree,  we  submit  that  such
statute  does  not  alter  the  rules  of  pleading;  that  its
whole  object  is  to  make  old  forms  of  pleading  cover
cases  not  theretofore  included  in  them;  and  that  to  at-
tribute  to  it  the  effect  of  requiring  indictments  to  be
more  specific  than  before,  is, so  far,  to  defeat  its  object,
which  is  to  diminish  the  scope  of  technical  objections.

b.  The  means  by  which  the  conspiracy  was  to  be  ren-
dered  effectual  are  specified  in  the  statute  by the  words
The  indict-
‘‘injure,  oppress,  threaten,  or  intimidate.”
ment  copies  these  words  conjunctively.

Here,  again,  it is conceivable  that  the  defendants  pro-

posed  to  use  all  of these  means,  and  not  one  only.

But  a  question  is  made  as  to  the  certainty  of the  above
Itis  suggested  that  the  prosecution  ought
expressions.
to  have  given  a detail  of the acts  relied  upon  as  “injury,”
&e.,  and  thus  to  have  allowed  the  court  to  say  whether
the  detail  amounted  in judgment  of law  to  tyury,  &e.

The  authorities  recognize,  at  this  point,  a  distinction
betwixt  the  certainty  required  in cases  of conspiracy,  and
that  required  in  cases  of  executed  violence.
Less  cer-
tainty  is  needed  in  the  former  class  of cases.  (Arch.  Cr.
Pl.,  [1871,]  55  and  9359;  Mulcahy  vs.  The  Queen,  Law
Rep.,  3  Eng.  and  Irish  Ap.,  306.)

For,  in  the  latter  class,  the  pleader  ean  always  inform
himself  of the  specific  acts  of injury,  oppression,  &c., done

by the  defendant.

A pete

1%

spiracy,  which  in  its  most  definite  form  rests  in  resolu-
tions,  written  or  spoken,  (Hunt’s  Case,  4  Mete.,  Mass.,  p.
125,)  the  conspirators  have  expressed  their  intentions  as
to  the  means,  by the  very  words  employed  in  the  indict-
ment!

We  are  not  understood  to  speak  here  of  those  sorts  of
conspiracy  where,  as  by  the  act  of  1867,  ch.  170,  the
law  requires  an  overt  act  in  order  to  complete  the  offense.
(14  Stat.,  484.)

Common-law  conspiracies  are  said  to  be of two  classes,
i. é.,  either  to  eftect  an  illegal  (not  necessarily  criminal)
purpose,  or  to  effect  a  legal  purpose  by illegal  means.
(Hunt’s  Case,  ubi  supra,  p.  123;  Queen  vs.  Warburton,
Law  Rep.,  1 Cr.  Cas.  Reserved,  274.)  As  the  present  is
a  conspiracy  created  by statute,  we  are  concerned  in  the
distinctions  observed  as  to  these  classes,  only  in  a  sub-
ordinate  degree.
To  make  out  the  offense,  both  the  ob-
ject  and  the  means  must  be  those  which  are  specified  in
the  act,  and  therefore  it  may  be  more  accurate  to  say
that  statutory  conspiracies  make  a  third  class.

The  reason  for  holding,  in  such  common-law  conspira-
cies  as  aim  at  a  legal  purpose  by illegal  means,  that
such  means  must  be  specified,  in order  that  the court  may
see  that  they  are  illegal,  does  not  apply  to  such  statutory
conspiracies  as  designate  both  the  objects  and  the  means
upon  which  they  operate.
In  the  latter,  whether  the
object  be  legal  or  illegal,  the  means  must  be stated ; 7. e.,
the  description  in  the  statute  must  be followed,  and  that
description  is complex,  viz:  not  that  what  is  punishable
is a  conspiracy  to  effect  a  certain  object,  but  a  conspiracy
to  effect  that object  in  a certain  way.

In  the  present  case  the  object  mentioned  in  the  act  is,
in  the  general  sense  of  the  word,  illegal,  and  so  also  are

2 CR

18

the  means;  even  supposing  them  not  to  be  indictable  in
the  courts  of  the  United  States.

We  submit,  therefore,  that  this  is a  matter  to  be  dis-
cussed  by the  light  of  the  rules  for  criminal  pleading  as
regards  statutory  offenses,  and  as  regards  conspiracy  in
general,

As  to  statutory  offenses,  the  only  general  rule,  as
this  court  has  said,  is, to allege  the offense in the very  terms
of the  statute.
(12 Wheat.,  p.  474.)  The  cases  not  inclu-
ded  under  this  principle  establish  no  general  rule  ;  on  the
contrary,  the course  has  been  to  leave  every  class  of them  to
be  decided  very  much  upon  its  own  peculiar  circumstances.
(Ibid.)

We  will,  theretore,  call  attention  to  such  precedents  in

pleading  as  seem  nearest  to  that  before  us.

3

Among  the  very  numerous  cases  which,  under  the
guidance  of  text-books  and  digests,  we  have  seen,  none
seems  to  be  more  like  the  present  than  that  of  Regina
vs.  Rowlands  and  others,  (9 Eng.  L.  and  E.,  287,  and  17
A.  and  E.,  N.S8.,  671,  [3 Wharton,  2323  ;] Paley  Convic-
tions,  209,  n.;  Arch.  Cr.  Pl.,  [1871,]  939,)  decided  in  1851
by the  Court  of  Queen’s  Bench,  (Campbell,  Patterson,
Coleridge,  and  EKarle,)  upon  a  motion  in  arrest,  after  an
elaborate  argument  for  the  defendant,  and  an  advisart,
the  Lord  Chief-Justice  delivering  the  opinion.

It  was  an  indictment  for  Conspiracy  at  common  law
to  commit  acts  rendered  criminal  by the  6th  George  IV,
There  were  twenty  counts;  upon  the  six-
ch.  29,  s.3.
teenth,  seventeenth,  and  nineteenth  of  which, after  inti-
mation  by the  court  that  they  might  be  too  vague,  a
nolle prosequi  was  entered.
The  other  counts  were  held
to  be  “  perfectly  good.”

19

The  act  in question  provides  that  “if  any  person  shall,
by violence  to  the  person  or  property,  or  by threats  or
intimidation,  or  by molesting  or  in  any  way  obstructing
another,  force  or  endeavor  to  force  any  journeyman,
manufacturer,  workman,  or  other  person  hired  or  em-
ployed  in  any  manufacture,  trade,  or  business,  to depart
‘from  his  hiring,  employment,  or  work,  or  to  retarn  his
work  before  the  same  shall  be  finished,  or  prevent  or
endeavor  to  prevent  any  journeyman,  manufacturer,
workman,  or  other  person  not  being  hired  or  employed
from  hiring  himself  to,  or  from  accepting  work  or  em-
ployment  from  any  person  or  persons;”  *  or  if any  person
Shall  by violence  to  the  person  or  property  of  another,
or  by threats  or  intimidation,  or  by molesting  or  in  any
way  obstructing  another,  force  or  endeavor  to  force  any
manufacturer  or person  carrying  on  any  trade  or  business,
to  make  any  alteration  in  his  mode  of  regulating,  man-
aging,  conducting,  or  carrying  on  such  manufacture,
trade,  or  business,  or  to  limit  the  number  of his  appren-
tices,  or  the  number  or  description  of  his  journeymen,
workmen,  or  servants;  every  person  so  offending,  or
aiding,  abetting,  or  assisting  therein,  being  convicted,”
&c., (summarily  before  justices,)  shall  be imprisoned,  &ce.
(See note  tol7  A.  & E., N.  8., (172  B,)  p.  678.)

The  indictment  charged  that  the  defendants  conspired
(1) by molesting  certain  workmen  hired  by R.  P.  and  G.
H.  P.  in their  trade,  &c.,  to force  and  endeavor  to  force
said  workmen  to  depart  from  their  hiring,  (2) by using
threats  to,  (3)  by  intimidating  said  workmen  to  force
them,  &c.,  (4) by the  molestation  of, &c.,  (5)  by threaten-
ing,  (6) by intimidation.

This  will  serve  as  a  sample  of  the  various  counts,
except  that  the  14th  and  15th,  added  to  the  means  men-

20

tioned  in other  courts—“  intoxicating ;” and  the  18th—
“intoxicating,  and  thereby  rendering  senseless.”

The  case  was  argued  for  the  defendants  by the  attor-
ney-general,  Sir  A. Cockburn,  and  by four  other  counsel.  .
The  argument  is  instructive  both  for  what  was  said  by
the  counsel  and  for  what  was  suggested  in  interruption
by the  court.  Some  of  the  points  were,  (1)  that  “  mo-
Jesting”  was  no  word  of art,  and  therefore  that  the  acts
of  molestation  should  have  been  set  out;  (2)  that
‘“‘threats”  was  liable  to  the  same  objection,  and  unless
it was  shown  what  the threats  were,  it would  be presumed
that  they  were  harmless;  (3)  so  for  “intimidating,”
which,  in  O’Connell’s  case,  (II.  Cl.  &  F.,)  had  been
decided  to  be  too  general;  (4) so  also  “  obstructing.”

The  court  said,  (9  Eng.  L.  &  E.:)  “The  principal
ground  of  objection  was,  that  they  do  not  set  out  the
means  and  nature  of  the  molestation  or  intimidation
charged.  We  think  that  is quite  unnecessary.
These
counts,  in  fact,  follow  the  words  of  the  statute  which  is
thus  shown  to  have  been  infringed,  and  these  words
have  a  legal  meaning  stamped  upon  them.  A  distine-
tion  may  be  made  between  a  conviction  for  doing  the
acts  prohibited  by the  statute  and  an  indictment  for  a
conspiracy  which  charges  those  acts  as  the  means  by
Here  the  offense
which  another  offense  is  carried  out.
consists  of a  conspiracy  to  violate  an  act  of  Parliament,
which  is an  offense  at  common  law;  and  the  acts  pro-
hibited  by  the  statute  are  charged  as  the  means  by
which  the  conspiracy  is  to  be  effected.  We  think  that
the  means  so  alleged  are  sufiiciently  specified,”  &c.

The  only  points  in which  this  case  differs  from  ours
do not  affect  its  value  as  an  authority.
For  conspiracy
at  common  lace  to  do  an  act  criminal  by statute,  such  act

— e
———

\ . ere

21

being  defined  by the  statute  as  the  effecting  of a  certain
end  by a  certain  means,  seems  to  differ  in  nothing  ma-
terial  as  regards  the  rules  of  pleading  from  conspiracy
created  by a  statute  that  makes  a  similar  definition  of the
object  intended.  We  have  met  with  no  authority,  and  no
reason  suggests  itself,  for  applying  different  standards
of  certainty  in  these  cases.

That  the  case  before  Lord  Campbell  was  a  misde-
meanor,  while  the  present,  when  instituted,  was  a  felony,
is not  a  matter  of  importance  in  this  connection.
In
this  connection,  it will be borne  in mind  that  conspiracy,
whether  misdemeanor  or  felony,  is  a  quasi  infamous
offense,  and  originally  at  common  law  was  followed  by
‘¢  villainous  judgment,”  very  much  as  is  inflicted  here
by the  Enforcement  act.

(Ch.  Cr.  Pl.,  3d,  1144.)

The  suggestious in Staats’  case  (8 How.,  4) render  it
doubtful  whether  any  distinction  in  pleading  exists  be-
tween  misdemeanors  and  felonies  in  the  courts  of  the
United  States—at  all  events, excepting  that  which  is in-
volved  in  the  rather  obscure  rule  that  courts  are  never
disposed  to  be  as  strict  as  to  less  offenses  as  they  are  in
reference  to  those  of greater  consequence.
As  an  illus-
tration,  we  submit  that  the  rule  of  pleading  as  to  the
offense  under.  consideration  remains  under  the  Revised
Statutes  as  it  was  by the  act  of  1872,  the  punishment
being  the  same  although  the  offense  is  no  longer  a  felony.
As  regards  statutory  offenses,  we  have  met  with  no
rule  defining  a  difference  between  pleading  in  misde-
meanors  and  in felonies,  (except  formerly  as  to  the  word
felonious,)  although  here  and  there  the  courts  have  called
attention  to  the  fact  that  the  case  before  them  was  a
misdemeanor,  as  affording  some  reason  for  their  decision.
We  submit  that  where  the  case  is  one  of  conspiracy,
